<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14439064763442445018">Young Tristran Thorn has lost his heart to beautiful Victoria Forester. But Victoria is cold and distant--as distant, in fact, as the star she and Tristran see fall from the sky on a crisp October evening. For the coveted prize of Victoria's hand, Tristran vows to retrieve the fallen star and deliver it to his beloved. It is an oath that sends the lovelorn swain over the a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14439064763442445018">Young Tristran Thorn has lost his heart to beautiful Victoria Forester. But Victoria is cold and distant--as distant, in fact, as the star she and Tristran see fall from the sky on a crisp October evening. For the coveted prize of Victoria's hand, Tristran vows to retrieve the fallen star and deliver it to his beloved. It is an oath that sends the lovelorn swain over the ancient wall dividing the village from the adjacent meadow, and propels him into a world that is strange beyond imagining. But Tristran is not the only one seeking the heavenly jewel. There are those for whom it promises youth and beauty, the key to a kingdom, and the rejuvenation of dark dormant magics. And a lad compelled by love will have to keep his wits about him to succeed and survive in this secret place where fallen stars come in many guises--and where quests have a way of branching off in unexpected directions, even turning back upon themselves in space and in time.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14439064763442445018">...more</a>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">There are some adult themes and some mild violence, but nothing gratuitous. The story reads like a fairy tale and reminds me a lot of The Princess<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">There are some adult themes and some mild violence, but nothing gratuitous. The story reads like a fairy tale and reminds me a lot of The Princess Bride. I'd say the book is appropriate for pre-teens but maybe not for small children.<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_4479913">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4479913" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/241981-l-h" class="left imgcol" title="L.h."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1221440893p2/241981.jpg" alt="L.h." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/4479913?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 08, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/241981-l-h" class="user" title="L.h.">L.h.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780061142024" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">dreamers</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/241981-l-h?shelf=fantasy-and-science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-and-science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/241981-l-h?shelf=fiction-and-literature" class="actionLinkLite">fiction-and-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4479913">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2719944992929272744">Dear Mr. Gaiman,<br /><br />Damn you. Damn you straight to hell. You've written beautiful faerie stories in your plainspoken postmodern prose, and left my own projected frontiers woefully trodden. It has nothing to do with your brilliance. Had I been born before you I would most likely be the one writing clever novels about fallen stars and sly gods. I would've, I swear!<br /><br />But instead, I was born forty years too late, and your Faerie, Neil, -do you mind if I call you Neil? Your Faerie, like all of your creatio</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2719944992929272744">Dear Mr. Gaiman,<br /><br />Damn you. Damn you straight to hell. You've written beautiful faerie stories in your plainspoken postmodern prose, and left my own projected frontiers woefully trodden. It has nothing to do with your brilliance. Had I been born before you I would most likely be the one writing clever novels about fallen stars and sly gods. I would've, I swear!<br /><br />But instead, I was born forty years too late, and your Faerie, Neil, -do you mind if I call you Neil? Your Faerie, like all of your creations, is a perfectly plausible reality, praised by literary critics, the literate's dollar, and even the behemoth Movie Adaptation. Your prose is simple, if you'll pardon my saying so, not elevated, with exotic adjectives, but simple and modern, easily accessible, solid, quality prose. This reflects nothing upon you, of course -your authorship is the perfect marriage of your own writing talent and our modern culture. Shakespeare's work seems ridiculously complicated to us now, but he wrote for the masses, just like you. Our masses do not value rhetorics, metre, or internal rhyme, or I'm sure you'd write with such tools. <br /><br />No, Neil, you write with one very powerful tool -distilled imagination. Escapism, unreality, creativity, novelty, and all their side effects. When the surrounding world seems unbearable, the ever-growing fetus of imagination is hope. In slavery, in misery, in poverty, in isolation, a person can escape into the brighter day of dreams, or the unknown future, or the magic of Faerie.<br /><br />And in a world where the demons in the closet are thrust into a florescent laboratory, where telescopes and cameras record the crags and terrain of places where once there were dragons, where we identify which crannies of the human mind are responsible for fear, or love, or sorrow, and even plot out their corresponding hormonal compositions for manipulation and control... perhaps in such a world we must desperately escape into the unexplorable reaches of Faerie. <br /><br />The rules are simple there. Help anyone in need, and graciously accept their payment. Stick with unerringly polite manners, but never let down your guard. You need a sharp wit to go with a good heart. Never eat faerie food.<br /><br />Popular vote does not elect poor leaders in Faerie. Weapons are easily recognizable, not hidden in letters or water bottles. Death stands tall with his scythe and cowl instead of creeping behind health care bills and drug overdoses, and when he does come, death was either justified, or will be avenged. Villages in Faerie are rarely overrun with "revolutionaries" toting machine guns or skeletal toddlers with distended bellies. The old do not die alone there. Despite enchanted princes and disguised witches, Faerie makes more sense than our world, and if it doesn't, it's alright. It's Faerie. <br /><br />So we escape into your novels, Neil, and into the worlds of Harry Potter and Lyra Silvertongue, and we love you for it (or in my case, hate you). We are too jaded to suspend disbelief, yet we ache for magic. We won't creep into a darkened room for the show, but demand that you blossom crystal snowdrops here in Times Square, your right hand outstretched for our inspection while your left clutches a Starbucks cup. We cannot leave our world for Faerie anymore, so you bring it to us in pleasurable words, easily explainable, hidden just beyond an English countryside wall, or under our feet, or in corners we don't examine too closely.<br /><br />Please keep it up. <br /><br />Sincerely,<br />L.H.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2719944992929272744">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_11572046">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11572046" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/171079-jen" class="left imgcol" title="Jen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1259203056p2/171079.jpg" alt="Jen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/11572046?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 06, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/171079-jen" class="user" title="Jen">Jen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780061142024" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Die-hard Neil Gaimon Fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/171079-jen?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer11572046'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11572046">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1593718632163011232">A friend gave me this book and I decided to read it before going to see the movie, since I'd heard so many rave reviews of the film. If the movie hadn't been my carrot, though, I never would have made it through the first chapter, let alone the entire story. Let me say that I adore the fantasy genre (check my book list), so this is not outside my interest. However, in attempting to write a "fairy tale for adults" Gaimon completely missed the mark. Apparently his idea of what makes a story "for a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1593718632163011232">A friend gave me this book and I decided to read it before going to see the movie, since I'd heard so many rave reviews of the film. If the movie hadn't been my carrot, though, I never would have made it through the first chapter, let alone the entire story. Let me say that I adore the fantasy genre (check my book list), so this is not outside my interest. However, in attempting to write a "fairy tale for adults" Gaimon completely missed the mark. Apparently his idea of what makes a story "for adults" is generous sprinklings of gore, violence, and sex. Considering that the rest of the story is written with child-like simplicity these sections are jarring, to say the least. When I stumbled across the first somewhat graphic sex scene within a few dozen pages, I literally stopped to double check what book I was reading. Call me a prude, but I don't think words like "nipple" and "thrust" really flow well in a fairy tale. Also, I defy any <a title="Fantasy Lover (Dark-Hunter, #1) by Sherrilyn Kenyon" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/84136.Fantasy_Lover__Dark_Hunter___1_">fantasy lover</a> to not flinch in horror at Gaimon's brutal and gore-spattered murder and subsequent dismemberment of the heroine's unicorn. "Pooling bodily fluids"? No, thank you.<br /><br />Still, it's not so much the sex &amp; violence that disturbed me (although if I liked that kind of thing I wouldn't be reading fantasy); it was that they were included in a tale lauded for its childlike adventure and whimsy. Even the description here on goodreads compares it to Princess Bride &amp; the Neverending Story - an insult beyond comprehension to works which demonstrate that, with skill, an author can in fact write a fairy tale for adults without employing R rated tactics. <br /><br />Lest you think I am allowing several instances of violence and sex to ruin my opinion of the entire work, let me address the actual plot line and writing. Gaimon can write, obviously, but the plot is riddled with fantasy travel cliches (oh, he slept in a hayloft? How original!) and tiresome caricatures. The hero is an unsympathetic clod, the star is referred to almost exclusively as "the star", rendering her more an inanimate object than a living being, and the rare nuggets of interest, such as the lightning pirates and the mysterious "kingdom brotherhood" are glossed over in a few paragraphs. And for a book that does not flinch from sex, where is the romance between the star &amp; hero? We endure their pedantic bickering the entire book, only to be rewarded with a passionless declaration of love betwixt them by the end. <br /><br />The ending is predictable yet less than satisfying, since the evil witch queen escapes justice and ultimately the star faces a lonely immortality bereft of her love. Not exactly the stuff happy endings are made of.<br /><br />UPDATE: I finally saw the movie, and let me say how grateful I am that it is NOTHING like the book! Other than borrowing the majority of the plot &amp; character names, the movie is night &amp; day different, employing a vast amount of humor &amp; charm where the book was violent and grim. I only wish my perspective had not been tainted by the book!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1593718632163011232">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_156414717">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156414717" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine" class="left imgcol" title="Kirstine"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1377246513p2/5172557.jpg" alt="Kirstine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/156414717?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 02, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine" class="user" title="Kirstine">Kirstine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/818478.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780755322824" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=adventure" class="actionLinkLite">adventure</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=1-men" class="actionLinkLite">1-men</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer156414717">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10980208197015381895">This is the one case, the ONLY case (so far) (edit: not anymore, Bridget Jones gets the honor too), where I prefer the movie to the book. I know it's a sacrilege and you can all burn me at the stake, but it is nonetheless the truth. It's also one of the few times I watched the movie before reading the book, simply because I had no idea the book existed. And I loved the movie. I mean, really, really loved it. <br /><br />So of course when I discovered it was based on a book, I rushed to get it. Now, please d</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10980208197015381895">This is the one case, the ONLY case (so far) (edit: not anymore, Bridget Jones gets the honor too), where I prefer the movie to the book. I know it's a sacrilege and you can all burn me at the stake, but it is nonetheless the truth. It's also one of the few times I watched the movie before reading the book, simply because I had no idea the book existed. And I loved the movie. I mean, really, really loved it. <br /><br />So of course when I discovered it was based on a book, I rushed to get it. Now, please don't get me wrong, it's a good book. It's a very very good book, and I love how Neil Gaiman tried (and succeeding at) creating a dark fairytale. Because it is very much a fairytale, except it's probably not so much for kids, as it is for adults. It shows us that fairytales aren't all glitter and roses, and good vs. evil, with happy endings for all the good guys. It's a more realistic fairytale. <br />That seems like such a contradiction, and few other people than Neil Gaiman could make it work (so thank god he's the one who wrote it). <br /><br />The problem is that having watched the movie I <em>wanted</em> a fairytale. All the things I loved the best about the movie, weren't in the book (and it's usually the other way around, I was surprised too). I mean, no Captain Shakespeare? No happy stars-in-the-sky ending? The movie was such a feel-good one and the book? Not so much. <br /><br />Had I done it in the proper order (book then movie) I'm absolutely certain I would have felt differently about it, but that's not how it is. Both book and movie are amazing in their own right, and perhaps I shouldn't even compare them, but ah, too late. <br /><br />Still, this is a great book and I really enjoyed it. So don't rid yourself of the chance to get to know some fantastic characters and go on a proper fairytale adventure with no risk of sugar exposure.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10980208197015381895">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_650280835">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/650280835" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may" class="left imgcol" title="Emily May"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1402215455p2/4622890.jpg" alt="Emily May" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/650280835?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 12, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may" class="user" title="Emily May">Emily May</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/818478.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780755322824" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2013" class="actionLinkLite">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer650280835">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1064108878330119024">The more Gaiman I read, the more I understand why people are so caught up in the magic he wields. Because that is basically what he does. He's not an author, he's a magician, painting magic pictures of rich, exciting worlds that come to life so quickly. Worlds that somehow seem complexly developed after just two chapters of Gaiman's writing. Gaiman is simply a master storyteller. He creates moods that permeate entire novels and, whether you happen to be reading his adult or young adult works, he</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1064108878330119024">The more Gaiman I read, the more I understand why people are so caught up in the magic he wields. Because that is basically what he does. He's not an author, he's a magician, painting magic pictures of rich, exciting worlds that come to life so quickly. Worlds that somehow seem complexly developed after just two chapters of Gaiman's writing. Gaiman is simply a master storyteller. He creates moods that permeate entire novels and, whether you happen to be reading his adult or young adult works, he makes you feel like a child wandering through a wardrobe into a world of possibility, or perhaps slipping through the invisible barrier of platform 9 3/4 and discovering the world is more than you could ever have imagined. What Gaiman does with his magic, is build timeless fairytales that speak to people of all ages.<br /><br /><a title="Stardust by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust">Stardust</a> is just one example of Gaiman's creativity. It is nothing like the hauntingly nostalgic <a title="The Ocean at the End of the Lane by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15783514.The_Ocean_at_the_End_of_the_Lane">The Ocean at the End of the Lane</a> or the eerily fantastical subterranean London of <a title="Neverwhere by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14497.Neverwhere">Neverwhere</a> or the ghostly coming of age story in <a title="The Graveyard Book by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2213661.The_Graveyard_Book">The Graveyard Book</a>, but it has the stamp of Gaiman all over it. His style is present from the very first chapter when a young man falls madly in lust with a Faerie girl and his passion results in the birth of Tristran Thorn. The tale only gets wilder, more exciting and more adventurous from there. A grown Tristran attempts to win the hand of his love by crossing the wall into the land of Faerie to retrieve a fallen star. But, as these things go, it doesn't turn out to be a simple task and, if Tristran even makes it back alive, he is certain to be a very different man to the one who left.<br /><br />However, as myself and others have done, it is very easy to feel the need to compare Gaiman's books to popular children's classics (I started the first paragraph by doing so) but <a title="Stardust by Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust">Stardust</a> is not a children's book. At a stretch, it could be called a young adult book but I'm tempted to play on the safe side and call it "adult". There's a sex scene in chapter one that is quite graphic. Not fifty shades of faerie, but still quite graphic. Plus there's some violence and gore that may put you off if you are looking for a light, fluffy fantasy read. In fact, I've read a bunch of GR reviews where the reader hated it because they'd read the blurb comparing it to <a title="The Princess Bride  by William Goldman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21787.The_Princess_Bride_">The Princess Bride</a> and <a title="The Neverending Story by Michael Ende" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27712.The_Neverending_Story">The Neverending Story</a> and seemed struck down in horror by the sex and violence. So, I'm warning you.<br /><br />The only thing more I can say about Gaiman is that I'm becoming a crazy fangirl and he needs to write more things and faster because soon I will have caught up with everything he's written. If you haven't yet, take a chance on him. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1064108878330119024">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1097894224">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1097894224" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/14596170-lola" class="left imgcol" title="Lola "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451614011p2/14596170.jpg" alt="Lola " /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1097894224?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/14596170-lola" class="user" title="Lola ">Lola </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780061142024" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=magic" class="actionLinkLite">magic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=witches" class="actionLinkLite">witches</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=adventure" class="actionLinkLite">adventure</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=family" class="actionLinkLite">family</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=prince-and-princess" class="actionLinkLite">prince-and-princess</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=love" class="actionLinkLite">love</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=kingship" class="actionLinkLite">kingship</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14596170-lola?shelf=3rd-person-pov" class="actionLinkLite">3rd-person-pov</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1097894224">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4819488777845178802"><strong>*3.5 of 5 shooting stars*</strong><br /><br /><img width="500" height="290" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1415164081i/11742039._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><blockquote> <strong><br />‘‘For a kiss, and the pledge of your hand,’’ said Tristran, grandiloquently, ‘‘I would bring you that fallen star.’’ He shivered. His coat was thin, and it was obvious that he would not get the kiss, which he found puzzling.</strong> <br /><br /><strong>The main heroes of the penny dreadfuls and shilling novels never had these problems getting kissed.</strong> <br /><br /><strong>‘‘Go on, then,’’ said Victoria. ‘‘And if you do, I will.’’</strong> <br /><br /><strong>‘‘What?’’ said Tristran.</strong> <br /><br /><strong>‘‘If you bring me that star,’’ said Victoria, ‘‘The one that just</strong></blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4819488777845178802"><strong>*3.5 of 5 shooting stars*</strong><br /><br /><img width="500" height="290" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1415164081i/11742039._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><blockquote>
  <strong><br />‘‘For a kiss, and the pledge of your hand,’’ said Tristran, grandiloquently, ‘‘I would bring you that fallen star.’’ He shivered. His coat was thin, and it was obvious that he would not get the kiss, which he found puzzling.</strong>
  <br /><br /><strong>The main heroes of the penny dreadfuls and shilling novels never had these problems getting kissed.</strong>
  <br /><br /><strong>‘‘Go on, then,’’ said Victoria. ‘‘And if you do, I will.’’</strong>
  <br /><br /><strong>‘‘What?’’ said Tristran.</strong>
  <br /><br /><strong>‘‘If you bring me that star,’’ said Victoria, ‘‘The one that just fell, not another star, then I’ll kiss you. Who knows what else I might do.’’</strong>
</blockquote><br />Unimpressively but well-fitting, it all started because of a <em>beautiful, irresistible</em> girl that unintentionally captured Tristran’s heart.<br /><br />Like his father, he ventures on the other side of the Wall. He is the second person known to ever do so but, unlike his parent, he is seeking THE falling star.<br /><br />This is my first read by <a title="Neil Gaiman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman">Neil Gaiman</a>, and I must admit that it went well enough. I was, I have to say, expecting this to leave me breathless and book hungover – since I always crave these fantasy reads – but it did not quite. Was still a good story nonetheless.<br /><blockquote>
  <strong>It has occasionally been remarked upon that it is as easy to overlook something large and obvious as it is to overlook something small and niggling, and that the large things one overlooks can often cause problems.</strong>
</blockquote><br />Let’s be honest here, this author has a <em>one of a kind</em> writing style. May I point out that this quote above was an entire sentence? You better not be annoyed by the length of those, if you want to fully enjoy it, I think. He also deals a lot with repetition but really as a writing style – nothing less.<br /><br />The main character, Tristran, was an easily likable one. However, he, in my opinion, was too calm…and so it felt like he was one-dimensional from time to time. The star, her, was a special character that I found strong and pure and wonderful to read about. I really thought her personality to be a good contrast with Tristran’s.<br /><br /><img width="500" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1415164201ra/11742055.gif" /><br /><img width="500" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1413554470ra/11528658.gif" /><br /><br />The three witches though were my favorite – in the book<em> and</em> movie. They were utterly entertaining and had an interesting role in the story. What was most fantastic about them was that they captivated me! Witches – and warlocks/wizards – have always been my favorite paranormal/surpernatural/fantasy creatures in the entire book world.<br /><br /><img width="500" height="200" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1415163163i/11741724.jpg" /><br /><br />The pacing was neither slow nor fast. I think it was just the right combination. There were moments, I admit, that were dragging or were just boring, but overall it was fine.<br /><br />When I think about the romance, I find that it was a lovely one…But not particular – if we forget that she is a star. I mean, she could have been a princess to capture and I would have felt the same. I guess the main problem was that it was predictable and underdeveloped, in my opinion, since there aren’t many dialogs and so not many chances for them to get to know each other better. Don’t get me wrong though, I did enjoy it! Simply not on a high rate.<br /><br />I would definitely recommend it to every fantasy/adventure/epic romance/witchery fan out there! A book that shall be read when in the mood and, I would also add that it would be preferable to watch the movie after reading the book…since it has - some - plot changes that may confuse you if you decide to pick the book after and not before.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4819488777845178802">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_11449250">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11449250" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/446464-kathryn" class="left imgcol" title="Kathryn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1316545868p2/446464.jpg" alt="Kathryn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/11449250?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 15, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/446464-kathryn" class="user" title="Kathryn">Kathryn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/222063.Stardust" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780380804559" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11449250">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5949704404844283615">Since I saw the movie before I read the book, I must preface my review with that fact since a comparison between the two was inevitable and, moreover, greatly influenced my opinion of the book. I loved the movie! I liked the book. Unlike most book-to-film adaptations, however, I felt that the movie had more character development and more details; and, indeed, more heart and more humor. I cannot objectively consider the merits of the book because I missed so many aspects of the movie-story as I r</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5949704404844283615">Since I saw the movie before I read the book, I must preface my review with that fact since a comparison between the two was inevitable and, moreover, greatly influenced my opinion of the book. I loved the movie! I liked the book. Unlike most book-to-film adaptations, however, I felt that the movie had more character development and more details; and, indeed, more heart and more humor. I cannot objectively consider the merits of the book because I missed so many aspects of the movie-story as I read. I just kept wondering if I would have liked the book better if I had read it first... <br /><br />That said, I give the book four stars because parts I felt were only three-star-worthy and parts were quite outstanding. Sometimes, it seemed Gaiman was struggling--that he was *trying* to be clever, imaginative, poetic, rather than simply *being* so. Other times, truly lovely and creative passages and ideas came across effortlessly. I felt the character development was lacking--as a cast of characters, he managed to assemble an interesting bunch, but no one was particularly special or deep. The love story was, to my romance-loving tastes, underdeveloped. And Gaiman seemed to the idea of "a fairytale for grown-ups" to mean that some rather vivid scenes of sex and violence (and the occasional curse word or crudity) were obligatory. Strangely (and thankfully!), however, I never felt he was mocking or cynical of the idea of a fairytale; indeed, his seemed lovingly crafted with due respect for magic and adventure and faerie-worlds. <br /><br />The basic premise of the book and movie are the same, but significant changes in plot and character development (even the cast of characters) provide a few surprises no matter which order one choses pursue. The movie is heartily recommended; the book is recommended, with reservation.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5949704404844283615">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_151534555">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151534555" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3270188-kat-kennedy" class="left imgcol" title="Kat Kennedy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1394913263p2/3270188.jpg" alt="Kat Kennedy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/151534555?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 20, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3270188-kat-kennedy" class="user" title="Kat Kennedy">Kat Kennedy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780061142024" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3270188-kat-kennedy?shelf=kat-s-book-reviews" class="actionLinkLite">kat-s-book-reviews</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3270188-kat-kennedy?shelf=fun-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fun-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer151534555">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9860942736960622150">Quick question: how many Daleks does it take to conquer Neil Gaiman?<br /><br />Answer: I don't know - since I am a blasphemous wench and have never seen a Doctor Who episode. Nor do I actually know what a Dalek is and what it does.<br /><br />To add to my nefarious ways, I'm also not a Gaiman fan (though not for lack of trying!) <br /><br />Clearly, whilst I am a scifi fan - I'm not the RIGHT kind of scifi fan!<br /><br />It's rather like two Star Trek fans meeting on the street:<br /><br />"So which episode of Enterprise is your favourite?" The first T</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9860942736960622150">Quick question: how many Daleks does it take to conquer Neil Gaiman?<br /><br />Answer: I don't know - since I am a blasphemous wench and have never seen a Doctor Who episode. Nor do I actually know what a Dalek is and what it does.<br /><br />To add to my nefarious ways, I'm also not a Gaiman fan (though not for lack of trying!) <br /><br />Clearly, whilst I am a scifi fan - I'm not the RIGHT kind of scifi fan!<br /><br />It's rather like two Star Trek fans meeting on the street:<br /><br />"So which episode of Enterprise is your favourite?" The first Trekker asks the second.<br /><br />"Oh, I only like Voyager and Deep Space Nine!" The second Trekker quickly replies.<br /><br />"I challenge you to Mortal Kombat!" screams the first Trekker, whipping out a Bat'leth, spittle flying from his mouth as fury transforms his features into something alien and cruel.<br /><br />Then they slap each other around for a while before going home.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Spock and Kirk in bath" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380346376i/700805.jpg" /><br /><em>One to write Spock and Kirk slash fiction and the other to masturbate to pictures of Jeri Ryan</em><br /><br />I truly believe you have to be the right kind of scifi fan to enjoy Gaiman to his fullest and unfortunately, like two strangers forced to interact occasionally, we just don't quite mesh together.<br /><br />I really enjoyed Stardust. Say what you want about Gaiman (no really, I already do) but he is a master of whimsy with a great imagination. Stardust has all the likable aspects of Neverwhere whilst managing to have a main character who is not a whiny, little annoying bitch! I was actually quite fond of this novel!<br /><br />Still, not a four star read from me. Having the story so heavily reliant on a magical fairy market, surprisingly similar to the one in Neverwhere lost some points with me. Of course themes can reoccur across novels but there was just too many tried and true mainstays of the fantasy genre in this book to really make it original and four-star worthy.<br /><br />As I've also found with other Gaiman novels is that he invests a great amount of interesting detail and depth into side characters, almost effortlessly, yet his two protagonists generally seem to lack the same treatment.<br /><br />Sure, Tristran and Yvaine grow and change. Sure they're definitely heads and shoulders better than a lot of characters written these days. But there's something vague and incomplete about them, their relationship together and decisions. They're still more puppets than characters and a great deal of Yvaine's character growth occurs off page. <br /><br />Some kind of explanation about the little man, the flying caption etc would have been nice as well. This was never expounded upon in the story. <br /><br />Over all, I enjoyed it. Not a perfect read, not a bad read either, but an enjoyable read.<br /><br />However, like the question of who would win: Neil Gaiman or the Daleks - well, I'm just ambivalent about the whole thing, really.<br /><br />Clearly I'm the wrong kind of Scifi fan.<br /><br /><span>[image error]</span><br /><em>Or maybe I'm the right kind of scifi fan... who's to say?</em><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9860942736960622150">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1275556751">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1275556751" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/27788046-bookworm-sean" class="left imgcol" title="Bookworm Sean"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1408905793p2/27788046.jpg" alt="Bookworm Sean" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1275556751?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 09, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/27788046-bookworm-sean" class="user" title="Bookworm Sean">Bookworm Sean</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780061142024" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=children-of-all-ages" class="actionLinkLite">children-of-all-ages</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1275556751">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17748810953869425137">I hate Tristan Thorn, though I do suppose that everybody has been in his shoes at one point in their life. Everybody was young once and everybody has been naively in love with someone they barely know. I can’t blame Tristan for his natural puppyish passions, he is only seventeen after all, but I can hate him for it nonetheless; he is completely unbearable at the beginning as his love-sick foolishness knows no bounds. Indeed, when Victoria Forester, the woman he <u>thinks</u> he in love with, agrees to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17748810953869425137">I hate Tristan Thorn, though I do suppose that everybody has been in his shoes at one point in their life. Everybody was young once and everybody has been naively in love with someone they barely know. I can’t blame Tristan for his natural puppyish passions, he is only seventeen after all, but I can hate him for it nonetheless; he is completely unbearable at the beginning as his love-sick foolishness knows no bounds. Indeed, when Victoria Forester, the woman he <u>thinks</u> he in love with, agrees to marry him if he fetches a fallen star, yes a fallen star, he childishly tries to retrieve it. <br /><br /><em>"He thought of Victoria’s lips, and her grey eyes, and the sound of her laughter. And to ignorant to be scared, too young to be awed, Tristan Thorn passed beyond the fields we know.</em> <br /><br /><img width="400" height="266" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1431459006p8/1181536.jpg" /><br /><br />In doing so he does grows as a person and almost redeems himself as he sees the errors of his ways. However, he is still an oaf and a self-obsessed idiot for most of the novel, which makes him quite unbearable as a person. Indeed, when he finally encounters the star he sees nothing but his ticket to getting between Victoria’s legs; instead of the wonder that is before him because the star is a magical being that belongs in another world. She attracts a whole host of problems, with Tristan’s lust for Victoria being the least of them. <br /><br />Also on route to claim the star for their own is a trio of princes, which ever one claims her earns the Kingship. Septimus, the youngest of the three, is power mad; he will stop at nothing to be the victor even if it means walking over the corpses of his fellow prince. However, a dark and more sinister threat approaches: the evil witch queen. If her and her sisters eat the heart of the star then their youth will be restored, and in doing so most of their already deadly powers too. <br /><br /><strong>But, Tristan is too unbearable</strong><br /><br />I do like Gainman’s writing, and I do like the idea behind this novel; however, I found Tristan to be an awful protagonist. He is not written badly nor is he a bad person, but I just found him annoying enough to affect my enjoyment of the novel. When you hate the protagonist so much it makes the story not as fun to read, and makes you want to throw it at the wall; it becomes frustrating rather than pleasant. I mean he is so much of a love sick puppy that <strong>it made me sick.</strong> I just wanted to slap him. He really is a repulsive guy:<br /><br /><em>“We shall visit my parents. I have missed them- although Tristan had barely given his parents a second thought on his journeying’s- then we shall pay a visit to Victoria Forester.”</em><br /><br />He just doesn’t see what’s in front of his face till the very end, and then it’s only when his first choice rejects him. Tristan doesn’t deserve the ending he gets in this book; he deserves a reality check. If Tristan was less of an idiot he would have annoyed me less and then I would have easily given this a four start rating, but, alas he is a moron. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17748810953869425137">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_462936665">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/462936665" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3344095-savanna" class="left imgcol" title="Savanna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1443373557p2/3344095.jpg" alt="Savanna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/462936665?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 12, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3344095-savanna" class="user" title="Savanna">Savanna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/818478.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780755322824" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3344095-savanna?shelf=speculative-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">speculative-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer462936665">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9294187878064265830">My high expectations for this book (the first I've read of Gaiman's) were badly disappointed. The writing was poor, the story cliché and shallow, and the content problematic. I've read that Gaiman is better with graphic novels, and that seems likely. He obviously has some talent, so I'm hoping this book is just a miss.<br /><br />One issue I had with Stardust was the writing itself. Gaiman tries to write an "adult fairy tale" with what I think are terrible results. The tone is light-hearted and sarcastic, b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9294187878064265830">My high expectations for this book (the first I've read of Gaiman's) were badly disappointed. The writing was poor, the story cliché and shallow, and the content problematic. I've read that Gaiman is better with graphic novels, and that seems likely. He obviously has some talent, so I'm hoping this book is just a miss.<br /><br />One issue I had with Stardust was the writing itself. Gaiman tries to write an "adult fairy tale" with what I think are terrible results. The tone is light-hearted and sarcastic, but it really isn't funny when it tries to be. Perhaps to make his story "adult," Gaiman is sure to include occasional scenes of sex and grotesque violence, but they seem awkward and out of place in the otherwise juvenile text. The plot and character development are extremely simplistic (as is the dialogue) in a way that would be better suited to a parable, but Stardust otherwise follows a more usual novel tone, so the suddenness and implausibility just come off as poor writing. The writing basically seems like a failed attempt at <a title="Piers Anthony" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8516.Piers_Anthony">Piers Anthony</a>'s Xanth novels that is neither clever nor funny nor original.<br /><br />This book makes heavy-handed use of basically every fantasy trope in existence. From the dry tone, it seems as though Gaiman's poking some deserved fun at them, but if there's meant to be an undertone of light-hearted criticism in Stardust, I couldn't find it. Why draw so heavily and randomly from these devices when they do nothing to round out Stardust's unimaginative world of Faerie, or to flesh out the flat characters and plot? By haphazardly including every familiar trope, Gaiman makes his story bland. I'm left wondering what the point was since it didn't seem to be satire but clearly tried for a sardonic approach. I'm sure he could have made an original, immersive, and interesting world filled with unique institutions and creatures, but he didn't even try. I don't get it.<br /><br />My third and biggest complaint with Stardust was just how utterly offensive the protagonist, Tristran, is. I don't think good books need to have likeable characters, but Tristran is so problematic yet presented so uncritically (none of the book's sarcasm seems directed at him and his goodness is emphasized throughout) that I get the feeling I was supposed to like him, or at least not find him intolerable. I'm surprised not to have read more criticism of Tristran since for me he was such a dickhole. I'll briefly sum up some of the story here to show why, so if you care about vague spoilers, ignore the rest of this review.<br /><br />Tristran sets out to capture a fallen star for the woman of his dreams. Who is she? What background have they shared? Why is this man so dedicated to her? All questions that could have fascinating answers, but irritatingly Gaiman employs the tired old story of a man being hopelessly in love with the most conventionally beautiful woman he knows entirely because of her beauty. (I'm again confused by this choice since Stardust is allegedly meant to shake up the genres of fairy tale and fantasy.) Pretty objectifying, sure, but he's young, so I cut him some slack initially. Then we read about how Tristran is just so darn ~*in love*~ that he regularly hangs out outside her window and watches her undress. This is not portrayed as weird or predatory; the book implies a "boys will be boys" attitude about this super creepy invasion of a woman's space. That's when I started to call shenanigans on the story, but it just got worse. <br /><br />Tristran finds out that the fallen star is actually a woman. He finds her crying because she broke her leg falling from the sky. (Yes, it seems the plot is derived from the world's worst pickup line.) So surely our hero, who is by all accounts a nice young man, and who is after all so very in love with his girl back home who doesn't want anything to do with him (poor Tristran! I can't imagine why) will help this damsel in distress! Wrong. He tricks her, literally shackles her with a magic chain, and FORCES HER TO WALK WITH HIM ON HER BROKEN LEG SO THAT HE CAN GIVE HER AWAY AS AN OBJECT SO THE HOTTEST GIRL IN TOWN WILL SLEEP WITH HIM. That actually happens. Cue a series of typical fantasy adventures. The star (who has a name, Yvaine, but is mainly just referred to as "the star" by protagonist and author alike) begs him to release her, tells him how miserable she is, and cries all the time. <br /><br />A true manic pixie dream girl, though, she's sure to tell Tristran to make sure he eats enough and doesn't wear himself out—fulfilling her secondary female role as indiscriminate caregiver alongside her primary role as piece of ass. But then she manages to escape, and Tristran feels betrayed and upset. Why won't the sexy ladies in his life just let him abuse them yet!? He suddenly comes to the realization that she's a human and that it's pretty fucked up to have enslaved her, but despite this abrupt stroke of sanity, he's determined to capture her again. He doesn't seem concerned about how fucked up he's being: he simply can't help it because he's in love (an answer everyone else and the author himself seem to accept). Cue more tired scenes with random descriptions of nipples and gore, and he's caught her again.<br /><br />Then as suddenly as every other development in the story, "the star" seemingly develops Stockholm syndrome when they conveniently arrive at their destination and finds herself now to be in love with our dear, admirable, and ever so refreshing protagonist. Too bad she's a present for the other sexy lady! What I presume was meant to be luckily for her, our poor Tristran is friendzoned again when the object of his infatuation confesses that she loves someone else. She doesn't even want her present (how ungrateful!). Dejected Tristran wanders back to Yvaine, where they abruptly declare their undying love for each other and proceed to live happily ever after. <br /><br />A dick through and through, Tristran also exerts his masculine, manly machismo when his estranged mother is freed from her own lengthy period of slavery to announce at the end that Tristran is actually heir to a powerful kingdom. (Of course he is!) Mom says how important it is to her that they go back in grandiose procession as a family after her forty years living as a slave in a wagon, but brave, manly, virtuous Tristran sticks up for himself with brave, manly virtue and tells his freshly liberated mom (also a perfect 10) that that's not his style (how humble!) and that he and his lady-object will show up sometime unannounced instead. He sure showed her! Way to stand up for yourself, Tristran!<br /><br />What I find most disturbing is that Tristran's blatantly misogynistic tendencies seem to be regarded as acceptable by Gaiman himself, while Yvaine's quantum of independence is laughed off as bitchiness. Tristran, who unabashedly objectifies women and literally enslaves Yvaine, is continually described as sentimental, passionate, and caring. Meanwhile Yvaine is described throughout as "hot-tempered" (even in the synopsis), yet the only things she does that aren't hyper-subservient are to insult Tristran (her slaver) and run away after making sure he's resting and has had enough to eat. This character is so mild-mannered and forgiving that she forsakes her independence (what's left of it) to spend her life with the man who so powerfully abused and degraded her. More than that, at the book's weak ending, when the witch who has been hunting her the entire time confronts her, Yvaine forgives the witch and KISSES HER ON THE FOREHEAD before wishing her well. Yet it is the absurdly gentle and obscenely forgiving woman character who is so stereotypically dismissed by Gaiman as uppity, emotional, and wild while the grotesquely selfish and openly cruel male protagonist is celebrated as a victim of passion. This is a sickening reinforcement of rape culture that is neither innovative nor subtle.<br /><br />So on top of the bad writing, confusing style, and general flatness, I found Stardust pretty offensive because of its treatment of women (I could go on about the lesser characters but I won't). It seemed like Tristran's misogynist exploits were all presented without a hint of reproach, as he won all the typical fantasy hero rewards and was described as sensitive and kind. I've definitely been super critical of him for this, but these weren't incidental issues within the story: they were central to the plot. Plus, in a book written by a contemporary author trying to challenge convention, it's extra difficult to let slide a story that's both poorly executed and deeply rooted in sexist stereotypes.<br /><br />BONUS BIGOTRY: I also noticed Gaiman twice used the pejorative racial slur "Gypsy" (which is never okay unless you happen to be Roma) and described Native Americans as "savages."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9294187878064265830">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/120294077?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 04, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2969647-maciek" class="user" title="Maciek">Maciek</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer120294077">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5655421382614883868">To tell the truth, I didn't believe it was possible. My copy of "Stardust" promises so much just by images on the cover - and the volume is so slim, barely reaching 200 pages. How will all these events and characters fit in such slim space? - I asked myself, and started reading on the evening of October, 2nd. By 4AM, October 3rd, I learned that not only it is possible, but also that Neil Gaiman is a talented, gifted writer with gorgeous imagination and invaluable, rare talent for recreating the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5655421382614883868">To tell the truth, I didn't believe it was possible. My copy of "Stardust" promises so much just by images on the cover - and the volume is so slim, barely reaching 200 pages. How will all these events and characters fit in such slim space? - I asked myself, and started reading on the evening of October, 2nd. By 4AM, October 3rd, I learned that not only it is possible, but also that Neil Gaiman is a talented, gifted writer with gorgeous imagination and invaluable, rare talent for recreating the noblest thing: magic.<br /><br />I think that the tale is really worth discovering for yourself, so I'll refrain from discussing the plot. Let me just say that few fantasy stories are as charming as "Stardust", which is a haunting journey from the border of the human reality into the magical land of Faerie. Gaiman mixes humor with seriousness, realism with fantasy, displaying skill rarely encountered that deserves to be admired and, quite simply, envied. His prose is simple and easily accessible, but in no way generic or cheap. The author's voice is never intrusive, and the tale moves at a surprisingly fast pace, switching effortlessly between several narratives which all are captivating and important - not a single moment is redundant. The descriptions are delicate and ethereal, and scenes are written in vivid detail - the tension at times is almost palpable.<br /><br />"Stardust" is a sublime, intriguing and simply beautiful fairy tale, full of compelling characters that the reader is sure to find himself caring strongly for, taking a journey most unique and exciting. Take it with them by reading this wonderful, memorable book destined to become a classic. Neil Gaiman is a marvelous storyteller who knows all his spells - prepare to be enchanted.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5655421382614883868">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1449291120">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1449291120" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/23304719-anne-anneshka" class="left imgcol" title="Anne (Anneshka)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451613103p2/23304719.jpg" alt="Anne (Anneshka)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1449291120?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 31, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/23304719-anne-anneshka" class="user" title="Anne (Anneshka)">Anne (Anneshka)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780061142024" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/23304719-anne-anneshka?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/23304719-anne-anneshka?shelf=peculiar-heroes-and-heroines" class="actionLinkLite">peculiar-heroes-and-heroines</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/23304719-anne-anneshka?shelf=magic" class="actionLinkLite">magic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/23304719-anne-anneshka?shelf=adventure" class="actionLinkLite">adventure</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/23304719-anne-anneshka?shelf=despicable-villains-and-antagonists" class="actionLinkLite">despicable-villains-and-antagonists</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/23304719-anne-anneshka?shelf=reads-of-2015" class="actionLinkLite">reads-of-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1449291120">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2880063385649611515"><strong>What a beautiful way to end my reading year!</strong><br />And that ending -<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451503175ra/17550214.gif" /><br /><br /><strong> <u>THE PLOT</u> </strong><br /><strong> <blockquote>The stars were laid out like worlds or like ideas, uncountable as the trees in a forest or the leaves on a tree</blockquote> </strong><br />A whimsical and atmospheric story which follows the adventures of an eighteen year old, <strong>Tristran Thorn,</strong> as he ventures out into the magical world of faerie-a world which borders his own little town, Wall. And why you ask did he undertake such a journey? Well, it's very simple. He did it for love. <strong> Stupid, blind, na</strong></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2880063385649611515"><strong>What a beautiful way to end my reading year!</strong><br />And that ending -<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451503175ra/17550214.gif" /><br /><br /><strong>
  <u>THE PLOT</u>
</strong><br /><strong>
  <blockquote>The stars were laid out like worlds or like ideas, uncountable as the trees in a forest or the leaves on a tree</blockquote>
</strong><br />A whimsical and atmospheric story which follows the adventures of an eighteen year old, <strong>Tristran Thorn,</strong> as he ventures out into the magical world of faerie-a world which borders his own little town, Wall. And why you ask did he undertake such a journey? Well, it's very simple. He did it for love. <strong> Stupid, blind, nauseating love.</strong> On his journey to retrieve a fallen star which his love, Victoria, demands in exchange for whatever he desires, Tristran encounters a lot of people, creatures, magical beings and objects, and a life changing truth.<br /><br /><strong>
  <u>MY THOUGHTS</u>
</strong><br /><strong>This book has one of the best prose I've read this year, </strong>and I am completely blown away by the splendor of Neil Gaiman's writing. From the very first page, I knew I and Neil Gaiman's books were meant to be, because it's not possible that the magnificence and artfulness of his word-weaving skills end with this book. It's not even remotely possible. And so I've decided to feed myself with his books come 2016. <strong>The writing was so scenic, </strong>and if I ever needed any confirmation of the power of words and their ability to create beautiful motion pictures in the mind, this book just did an amazing job of reaffirming that fact. And believe me, the easiness of the delivery of this picturesque tale had nothing, absolutely nothing to do with me having watched the movie already. I was making a movie of my own the whole time. <br /><strong> HOW I FEEL ABOUT NEIL GAIMAN </strong><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451503371ra/17550231.gif" /><br /><br /><strong>
  <u>WHAT BITES</u>
</strong><br /><strong>Tristran. </strong><u> Tristran is a born fool, and a consistent one at that.</u> He offered me nothing more than comedic relief. Imagine this: Tristran asks Victoria for a kiss, and for her hand in marriage. Yes, it's the silliest thing ever, considering he doesn't even know her that well, but, you'd think that for someone who the world revolves around, who is the epitome of beauty and sweetness, courtesy and kindness, or even tact, wouldn't be foreign traits. You'd think they'd be polite and gentle even if they don't feel the same way. But what does Victoria say?<br /><br /><em>Marry you? she repeated, incredulously. And why ever should I marry you, Tristran Thorn? What could you give me? </em><br /><br /><strong>STRIKE ONE </strong><br /><br />But Tristran doesn't relent. He promises her treasures and jewels from Africa and India -and something about the Nile. I couldn't be bothered to remember what. He says he'd travel the world for her. And my dear Vicky says:<br /><em><br />Be that as it may, little shop-boy and farm-boy, I shall not kiss you; neither shall I marry you. </em><br /><br /><strong>STRIKE TWO</strong><br /><br />BUT TRISTRAN LIKE I SAID IS A CONSISTENT BORN FOOL. But now he graduates from a consistent born fool into a groveler with no atom of dignity or self preservation. He's relentless in his declarations of love, so she sends him after a fallen star. A fallen star? Because you are who?<br /><br /><strong>STRIKE THREE</strong><br /><br />But my good man Tristran, agrees to go after it, agrees to go in search of the star in a land no one in Wall had ever dared to cross, except on the one market day when interactions between both worlds were allowed. So off goes Tristran through the gap in the wall that separates the two worlds: Faerie and human.<br /><strong>ME</strong><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380372701ra/726043.gif" /><br />I couldn't help but wonder at certain points, Is Tristran being portrayed as a caricature of something? I doubted the seriousness of his character. I laughed at his foolishness. And if I was giving ratings based on his character alone, this book would have been a two star for me. I mean everytime he opened his mouth I was just like: <br /><strong>I dare you</strong><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1422107739ra/13457344.gif" /><br /><strong> Go on, say it. Just try saying it.</strong><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451508265ra/17550576.gif" /><br />But ultimately, the rest of the characterization was splendid and I loved this story. And I couldn't have asked for a more enchanting or better ending. I guess that's it, goodbye to 2015.<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1430691568ra/14748316.gif" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2880063385649611515">...more</a>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1178538744" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/29931457-franco-santos" class="left imgcol" title="Franco  Santos"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1426036813p2/29931457.jpg" alt="Franco  Santos" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1178538744?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/29931457-franco-santos" class="user" title="Franco  Santos">Franco  Santos</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Paperback 9780061142024" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/29931457-franco-santos?shelf=libros-en-digital" class="actionLinkLite">libros-en-digital</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/29931457-franco-santos?shelf=le%C3%ADdos-en-ingl%C3%A9s" class="actionLinkLite">leídos-en-inglés</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1178538744">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2067025148482133483"><blockquote> <em>Every lover is, in his heart, a madman, and, in his head, a minstrel.</em> </blockquote>¡Qué libro! El quinto que leo de Gaiman y, como siempre, me deslumbró con su habilidad para mezclar una atmósfera oscura con una infantil y, hasta cierto punto, tierna. El universo de realidades que crea es de lo mejor que leo en el género. <br /><br />Al principio se me hizo un poco pesado y, aunque me duela decirlo, aburrido, pero conforme iba avanzando, me atrapaba más y más. También no saber bien qué era lo que sucedía fue una manera</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2067025148482133483"><blockquote>
  <em>Every lover is, in his heart, a madman, and, in his head, a minstrel.</em>
</blockquote>¡Qué libro! El quinto que leo de Gaiman y, como siempre, me deslumbró con su habilidad para mezclar una atmósfera oscura con una infantil y, hasta cierto punto, tierna. El universo de realidades que crea es de lo mejor que leo en el género. <br /><br />Al principio se me hizo un poco pesado y, aunque me duela decirlo, aburrido, pero conforme iba avanzando, me atrapaba más y más. También no saber bien qué era lo que sucedía fue una manera de mantenerme enganchado. Gaiman me iba dando de beber conocimiento a pequeños sorbos cuando yo quería saberlo todo. Y heme aquí, con el libro ya acabado. Y sí, estoy satisfecho. <blockquote>
  <em>He stared up at the stars: and it seemed to him then that they were dancers, stately and graceful, performing a dance almost infinite in its complexity. </em>
</blockquote>La trama me encantó. A pesar de que parte de una idea explotada en demasía, a medida que avanzaba me iba dando cuenta de que era mucho más de lo que aparentaba al inicio. Es un relato precioso, con un trasfondo cliché pero con un desarrollo único. Además la prosa del autor es un aliciente que no se puede ignorar: es fantástica. <br /><br />Como aspecto negativo puedo decir que me pareció bastante inverosímil la cadena de casualidades que unían a los personajes. Me resultaron excesivas. No niego que me gustaron mucho, no obstante, eso hizo que haya una cierta distancia entre el libro y yo, puesto que esas coincidencias me recordaban constantemente al hombre detrás de la historia, que quiso que todo fuera así para que esta pueda avanzar. <blockquote>
  <em>It's not hard to own something. Or everything. You just have to know that it's yours, and then be willing to let it go.</em>
</blockquote>Con respecto a la conclusión, me maravilló. Es forzada, sin embargo, habiendo leído las improbables eventualidades ya mencionadas, tomé como natural esa mano divina del escritor para que ese cierre pueda darse. Una vez aceptado aquello, solo me restó disfrutar. Y es por eso que me fascinó. Me dejó un sentimiento de añoranza y melancolía, algo que siempre valoro en un final. <br /><br />En fin. <em>Stardust</em> lo recomiendo sin lugar a dudas. Lectura ideal para ignorar por un buen rato la realidad y sumergirse en un mundo de magia, amor, esperanza, aventura y una estrella caída.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2067025148482133483">...more</a>
